 KUNGL. VITTERHETS HISTORIE OCH ANTIKVITETS AKADEMIEN STOCKHOLM REVELATIONES SANCTAE BIRGITTAS REVELACIONES LIB . VI EDIDIT BIRGER BERGH 
 
 
 
 
 SANCTA BIRGITTA REVELACIONES BOOK VI EDITED BY BIRGER BERGH 
 
 
 
 
 Published with the aid of grants from the Swedish Council for Research in the Humanities and Social Sciences BERGH, B., Sancta Birgitta, Reuelaciones, Book VI. 288 pp., Svenska Fornskriftsällskapet and Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och Antikvitets Akademien. Stockholm 1991. ISBN 91-971745-0-5. The present volume contains the first critical edition of the Latin text of St. Birgitta's (1303-73) Revelations, Book VI. The edition is based on a collation of eleven selected MSS. which, in the editions of other Revela- tions books, have proved to be of high quality. In one of the introducto- ry chapters, the relationships between these MSS. are accounted for, and a stemma codicum is presented. The archetype of the MSS., re- constructed as described in the chapter on principles of edition, is analysed in detail in a chapter on textual criticism. It appears to contain numerous errors, which are corrected in the edition. A glossary after the edition lists all such words as are either lacking in Georges' Handwörter- buch or appear there with a meaning not applicable in the text of the present edition. Indices of different kinds conclude the edition. Birger Bergh, Klassiska institutionen, Sölvegatan 2, S-223 62 LUND, Sweden. ISSN 0347-9145 ISBN 91-971745-0-5 Printed by Berlings, Arlöv, Sweden 1991

 Introduction The textual history of the Reuelaciones (=Rev.) of the Swedish saint, Birgitta Birgersdotter (1303-73, canonized in 1391), has been outlined by several scholars, the latest and most informative account being that of C.-G. Undhagen in his edition of Rev. I, pp. 1-37 (with references to other relevant literature). The sixth book, published here for the first time in a critical edition, was included in the very first handwritten edition of the Rev., published in 1377 by Alphonso, the Spaniard and former Bishop of JaU, whom Birgitta had repeatedly commissioned to be the editor-in-chief of her works. It is, therefore, not surprising that Alphonso, in his Epistola Solitarii-(=ES), dated by the modern editor A. Jönsson to 1375-76, refers several times to Rev. VI as weil as to other books within the Liber celestis (=Rev. I—VII). (One of the references to Rev. VI concerns ch. 101; see Jönsson, pp. 108, 206. This merits mention considering the misleading and, on several points, incorrect account given by Ekwall, p. 85 ff.) In this first Rev. edition, which Undhagen calls 'the first Alphonso redaction', Rev. VI contained 109 chapters. Chapters 110-122, being part of the `supplemen- tary material' in the Rev. corpus, i.e. such material as was either excluded by Alphonso or not available to him, were incorporated in later editions (see Undha- gen, p. 26 ff.). The supplementary material of Rev. VI comprises not only the chapters mentioned but also addiciones and declaraciones to a number of the other chapters, as will be accounted for below on p. 18 ff. Four chapters in Rev. VI, viz. 26, 41, 82 and 95, appear also as Rev. VIII 30, 44, 38 and 29, respectively. The reason for this is that Alphonso, when gathering material for the Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges (=Rev. VIII, preceded by his ES), took approximately half of the chapters from Rev. the other half consisting of revelations not included in these books (cf. Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 22 f.). In some MSS., Rev. VI 26, 41, 82 and 95 are omitted with a reference to the corresponding chapters in Rev. VIII; this is true of 26 in q, 41 in Fb F and 82 and 95 in q F (cf. Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 29 f.). Rev. VI contains both revelations experienced by Birgitta while in Sweden (1344-49) and such revelations as were given to her during her years in Italy (1350- 73). A survey of the material with local and chronological aspects taken into consideration is given by Lun&n, 3, pp. 9-20; cf. also Nyberg (1990), p. 36 f. When studying Birgitta's Revelations, it should be borne in mind that the extant Latin text is the result of Alphonso's revision of the translation by Birgitta's Swedish confessors, Prior Petrus and, to some extent, Magister Petrus, of the 
 
 
 
 
 12 Swedish text, which Birgitta normally wrote but sometimes dictated (see A&P, p. 84). In addition, much of the material is not Birgitta's at all. This is true of the many redactional notes, both at the beginning and at the end of various chapters; such notes are particularly characteristic of the addiciones and declaraciones. Moreover, in accordance with his editorial task, Alphonso not only divided the text into books and chapters but also gave a heading to each individual chapter (see Ex., prologus sec. v, § 3; Hollman, p. 114). Now and then these headings give information not found in the subsequent text, which implies that Alphonso had received supplementary explanations from the Swedish confessors, from Birgitta herself, or from some other source. An instructive example is 68:R: the death of a certain monk, indicated by the words quod prochdolor postea sic euenit, is not reported in the text, which only says (§ 5) nisi cito redierit ad monasterium. . .mor- ietur infra breuissimum tempus (a parallel is found in 25:R). It should be empha- sized that information included in headings and other redactional material in the Rev. is not always wholly reliable (cf. Rev. V, p. 18; Klockars 1966, p. 55). Occasionally, Alphonso himself underlines the hypothetical character of the interpretations he introduces. This is true of IV 72:R where, interestingly enough, Alphonso among other things suspects that Christ's words about Martha and Mary in the subsequent chapter in reality refer to Birgitta herself and her daughter Katherina, and in IV 81:R he reasonably suspects that the anonymous speaker of the following revelation is the Virgin Mary. In both cases, Alphonso uses the words vt credo, thus breaking the otherwise objective style of the headings. In referring to Birgitta's Reuelaciones on the following pages, I use the modern editions of Rev. I, V—VII, Ex., RS and SA. For references to Rev. IV and QO, I have gratefully had at my disposal the as yet unprinted editions by H. Aili and S. Eklund, respectively. Rev. II and Rev. III are referred to according to preliminary editions benevolently lent to me by C.-G. Undhagen and A.-M. Jönsson, respec- tively. Quotations from Rev. VIII were taken from the editio princeps, Ghotan 1492 (with a division into paragraphs made by me). 
 
 
 
 
 The Manuscripts The MSS. collated for the present edition were chosen with regard to the results obtained in my own analyses of the Rev. V and Rev. VII traditions and those obtained by Undhagen in his edition of Rev. I. On the whole, our analyses have led to the same conclusions, and to the extent that MSS. used by Undhagen and myself also appear in Eklund's editions of SA and RS, they are evaluated in roughly the same way. This means that the Liber celestis MS. tradition has been found to fall into two main branches, designated (3 and n, each with two sub- groups, called [31 and P2 respectively y and o. The resulting stemmata codicum of the different books published are, however, not identical, the most striking discrepancy being that r is a 1 MS. in Rev. VII but a t MS. in Rev. V, and whereas P was found to belong to the n group in Rev. VII, it belongs to the MSS. in other books. Both r and P were examined for the present edition, too, together with B F K 0 Y Ab Ib q v, but to these MSS. some are to be added which contain supplementary material only (cf. above, p. 11, and below, p. 18). As regards the lacuna in q, for which Flb was used, see below, p. 17. 1.(3- t There are a number of passages, in which B 0 P Y Ab r = p have one reading, whereas F K Ib q v (and, normally, Gh, the editio princeps) = n have another. The dependency of the former MSS. on a common source is demonstrated in the following selected cases, in which these MSS. seem to have a common error. In 9:8 dicit aliud ore et aliud habet in corde, the p MSS. add in before ore, but the phrase dicere (or loqui) in ore lacks parallels in the Rev. On the other hand, cases like Rev. I 48:23 aliud loquuntur ore et aliud habent in corde and A&P, p. 410 ore dicendo et non corde perpercisti jnimicis tuis indicate that the authentic reading is preserved in the n MSS. It would seem that the n MSS. have the correct reading also in 22:13 Debet enim primo auferre a se omnem superbiam et ostentacionem et assumere veram humilita- tem. Secundo auferat a corde suo omnem cupiditatem etc. Instead of a corde suo according to n Gh, the 13 MSS. have a se. The difference is most easily explained as a p error influenced by the preceding auferre a se; as for the n phrase, cf. III 21:18 petunt humiliter a Deo, vt ab eorum cordibus auferatur superbia et ambicio and VII 5:28 delectaciones internas a corde auferre. The n MSS. have in 15:1 the reading Si enim vellet esse meus , but here the (3 MSS. have the word-order Si vellet enim etc. Now, an immense amount of material (almost 1100 cases) indicates that, in the Rev., enim can be forced out of its normal 
 
 
 
 
 14 second to third place in the clause only if it is preceded by a prepositional phrase, by ut quid (=quare) or by the form est (in IV 92:1 enim is preceded both by a prepositional phrase and est: In eis est enim Pater etc.). The I word-order Si vellet enim has no parallel at all, whereas si enim followed by a finite verb is well established, e.g. I 53:26 where si enim vellem is unanimously handed down in the MSS. Thus the actual usage in the Rev. is in favour of the 3T reading. A comparable case is 35:41 Quid autem ego facturus sum etc. This is the n Gh reading, and the p MSS. have the word-order Quid ego autem etc. There are almost 1000 occurrences of autem in the Rev., and like enim it is regularly the second word of the clause and appears as the third word only if preceded by a prepositional phrase, or if the second word is est, e.g. RS § 55 Sciendum est autem etc. The p word-order in 35:41 lacks convincing parallels, but the sequence autem ego occurs rather often; from Rev. VI only one may quote 18:16, 19:16, 28:3 and 30:27 (the last case identical with 35:41). It should be made clear that if a clause starts with a prepositional phrase, then autem must be the third word. This is demonstrated in a vast amount of material, e.g. 34:4 In primo autem verbo. In accordance with this, the correct reading in 93:6 must be Post hec autem statim apparuit (n Gh), but here too the (3 MSS. B 0 Y r differ in reading statim autem. In this case, the word statim was omitted from P, and Ab has Post autem statim, but there is reason to believe that the f3 hyparchetype had the word-order statim autem. It would appear that the copyist of that hyparchetype was negligent in questions of word-order implying autem and enim, for in 36:11 there is again an abnormal 3 word-order: instead of Quomodo enim ego etc. in It and P, the other p MSS. have Quomodo ego enim etc. (cf. e.g. 112:1 Quomodo enim ego). In the last case discussed, P has the same correct reading as n, and there are parallels to this. In 31:35, for instance, Ideo voces hominum clamant super eum vindictam, angeli auertunt ab eo faciem, sancti fugiunt consorcium eius, the p MSS. have, instead of eum according to P n Gh, the reading eam. This reading was probably influenced by animam in the preceding paragraph (Habuit enim animam suam venalem), but it seems necessary to regard eam as an error considering the following eo which refers to the same person (and replaced, therefore, by ea in the 13 MS. 0). If these cases of identical correct reading in P and n were not caused by mere chance but express some sort of dependency, it should be possible to find cases in which P and n have the same erroneous reading as well. I have noted some cases of P It consensus against 13, viz. 9:R, 35:30, 52:26, 59:15 and 70:R, and at least in the last mentioned case it seems possible to argue that P n are wrong and f3 is right. The text runs, according to [3 and Gh: Et comminatur (sc. Christus) prelatis et clero cum districta iusticia terribiliter etc., but instead of iusticia, P and n have sentencia. It is of course impossible to maintain that districta sentencia would be quite out of place here, and one may compare our case with Rev. VIII 48:12 Deus mutat districcionem sentencie sue. It is, on the other hand, a fact that the combination districta sentencia does not otherwise appear in the Rev., whereas ES 6:92 fugerent 
 
 
 
 
 15 a terribili et districta iusticia diuina gives us not only a parallel of districta iusticia but also, in the word terribili, a counterpart to terribiliter in our case. One may add that similarities between ES 6:92 and the heading of Rev. VI 70 (according to p) are quite understandable considering the fact that Bishop Alphonso was the author of both texts (cf. above, p. 12). I am therefore inclined to regard the P n reading sentencia as an error; it may have been influenced by sentenciam earlier in the heading. It should be added that the list of headings included in some MSS. has (both in Tt and (3) iusticia, thus supporting the 13 reading in 70:R (see below, p. 22). The conclusions to be drawn so far from this analysis are that in Rev. VI, too, the MS. tradition is divided into two branches, [3 and n, furthermore that P, being dependent on both branches, is a contaminated MS. It remains, however, to be established whether there are errors binding the n MSS. against 13 (P), just as the [3 MSS. were demonstrated to have common errors in cases where TE or P n preserve the original wording. In 39:104 Rogo ergo te, fili carissime etc., the n MSS. (and Gh) add mi after fili. However, all MSS. have the reading dulcissime fili in § 106 without any mi, and this may be taken as an argument indicating that 3 P preserve the correct reading in § 104, particularly since the passage in § 106 can hardly have exerted any influence on the one in § 104; an influence in the opposite direction would be theoretically more understandable. In 10:12 there is a discrepancy in word-order: colaphizatus et consputus es is the reading of n Gh as compared to colaphizatus es et consputus in the other MSS., but as was pointed out in my edition of Rev. V, p. 44 f., the former reading is typical of a tendency in the TE recension to normalize the word-order. Other discrepancies between 13 (P) and It are found in e.g. 10:4, 11:R, 27:14, 29:4, 30:2, 35:10, 39:48, 52:15 and 53:1, and though it seems difficult to advance linguistic or contextual arguments as to which reading is correct in all individual cases, they nevertheless add to the general impression of a MS. tradition divided into two branches, 3 and It . 2. Pi - P2 A nutuber of cases prove that the p branch of Rev. VI contains two subgroups, in my material represented by P Y r = 131 and B 0 Ab = P2, respectively. Ömissions common to P Y r are found in 14:15 (a , before delectacione), 19:30 (meam), 24:16 (vnus), and in 26:10 these MSS. have (before correction) the reading animo (animi) instead of a nimio. Examples of errors binding B 0 Ab are 5:5 putredo instead of putrida, the addition of omnium before 8:11 sanctorum and the contextually impossible didicit for 52:18 didici. 3. P Y r 431) There is a close relationship between P and the uncorrected Y, as appears, for example, from the common omission of 19:31 peperi, 25:1 cantilenam and 52:39 
 
 
 
 
 16 excitant. However, within the [31 group there are also clear connexions between Y and r, and as examples of common errors in these two MSS. one may quote the omission of 10:R sponsa, 24:17 in and 46:R verbum. . .illud. In 22:8 we find seueritatem instead of austeritatem, in 32:19 consilia instead of consimilia, and further cases occur 50:10, 65:1.30 and 69:6. The cause of this problematic state of affairs, i.e. the existence of both P Y and Y r errors, is in all probability the contamination in P already accounted for. Since P contains material from the n branch, it is quite possible that the Y r errors were in the 131 hyparchetype as weil, but in that case they were removed from the P recension by the contamination in question. The errors in P Y, on the other hand, derive from a source upon which r does not depend. These errors make it impossible for P or Y to be the source of r. Besides, individual errors in P (e.g. the omission of 20:10 clamans in cruce) exclude the possibility that P was used as the exemplar of any of the other MSS., and the errors in Y (uncorrected) and r rule out either of these as the exemplar of P. There are a number of individual errors in r as weil (e.g. the omission of 39:31 quandoque eciam in ira alienando sua). Y was thus not copied from r — which, if Nordenfalk is right in dating Y to `before 1391' (p. 379), would have been impossible for chronological reasons alone (r written 1399) — but it would seem that r is the source of a number of corrections in Y, e.g. sic was added in Y2 r after 27:12 se, and in 43:4 the word sua (after colligit arma) was omitted from r and deleted by Y2. There is a theoretical possibility that the corrected Y was the primary source of the P recension, but this cannot be substantiated. The secondary source of this recension may be somehow connected with the y MSS.: common P y errors are e.g. patre instead of 34:20 Filio, cepit for 55:3 incepit, the addition of et in 58:14 and the reading nunciauit instead of 62:R annunciauit. The situation is thus very much the same as in the Liber questionum; cf. Rev. V, p. 55 f., cf. also Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 169 f. with n. 22. The MS. Y is of considerable interest due to the very fact that it once belonged to the monastery of St. Girolamo di Quarto, in which Alphonso spent his last years (cf. Jönsson, p. 81 f.). Also r is closely connected with this monastery, as appears from a note written after Rev. VI, fol. 118r: Explicit liber vj. scriptus et correctus ex originalibus quondam venerabilis heremite alfonsy quondam episcopi genensis. MCCCIC (twice) Janue in monasterio nostro sancti Jeronimy (cf. Bergh, Rev. VII, p. 36, and Undhagen, Rev. I, p.157). In my edition of Rev. VII, I had found some passages in which Y (32 seemed to be erroneous whereas r and It had the correct reading (p. 38 f.), and I advanced a hypothesis that the phrase scriptus et correctus etc. might imply a contaminated r1 text. As was pointed out by J.-O. Tjäder in NTBB 55,1968, p. 22, this hypothesis presupposes a questionable interpretation of the quoted words. Since the note in question is written immediately after Rev. VI, it is of course of particular interest to analyze the text and the corrections in this book. It should then be emphasized that, in Rev. VI, there are too many errors in r, both individual ones and errors in combination with Y, to make a very close relationship with the original codex of the Alphonsine redaction probable. There- 
 
 
 
 
 17 fore it seems advisable to judge the quality of the r text without any regards to the note after Rev. VI. It should, besides, be remembered that the note was not entered by the copyist who wrote Rev. VI, and furthermore that the copyist, as observed above (p. 13), used different MS. recensions for Rev. V and Rev. VII (cf. Rev. VII, p. 34 ff., Rev. V, p. 57 ff.). As regards the actual meaning of originale, it should also be borne in mind that the word does not necessarily correspond to our 'original' (for which authenticum is often used; cf. SG1) but very often, particularly in the plural form, has the meaning of 'complete works', as pointed out by J. de Ghellinck in ALMA 14,1939, p. 95 ff. 4. B 0 Ab 032) Since all the P2 MSS. have individual errors, no single one can have been the source of any of the others. Thus the words scienciam. . .maiorem were omitted from B in 30:23, in 0 there is an omission of 19:17 Ego. . .eos and among the numerous Ab errors one may refer to the omission of 14:2 animi and 18:10 et considera. The number of individual errors in Ab is in reality rather large, and it is tempting to disregard this MS. in the reconstruction of the (32 hyparchetype. I nevertheless decided to use it as a witness to an otherwise unrepresented group of MSS. (cf. Rev. VII, p. 50, and Rev. V, p. 67). 5. y - o As examples of errors binding the y MSS. together — in my material represented by Ib and v — one may quote the bewildering addition of homo dei in 6:R, furthermore the omission of 23:R Cui. . .peccata and of 42:7 et castitate. Neither MS. was used as the exemplar of any other, as appears from the Ib reading scilicet for 31:38 si and the omission in v of 29:8 quorum intellectus. . .suam. The existence of a common hyparchetype (o) of the MSS. F K q (and Gh) seems to be proven by cases like the omission of 5:8 in and 14:17 te (before ducet ad patriam), the addition of ibi after 107:3 veniunt and the reading loquetur instead of 4:3 loquatur. There is a long lacuna in q corresponding to 39:51-72:1, and here Fb was used instead (cf. Rev. V, p. 64, Rev. I, p. 215). Individual errors in these MSS. are, for instance, the omission of 23:16 quia. . .deberem in F, of 30:16 per amorem. . .amore Dei in K, of 23:23 pro latrone in q (and of 39:63 videmus et in Fb); thus none of these MSS. served as the exemplar of any other. Within the o MSS. there seems to be some relationship between K and q (Fb), as appears from the common omission of 35:35 mea and 36:5 erat, the readings sum for 19:24 sim and alii instead of aliis in 39:19. As regards other kinds of dependen- cy within the group (including Gh), the material is too vague to admit of any convincing conclusions. 2 — Birger Bergh 
 
 
 
 
 18 The results of this investigation into the Rev. VI MSS. are in accordance with what is known from other books within the Liber celestis. The MS. tradition is split up into two main branches, each with two subgroups, and the relations between the MSS. may be illustrated by means of the following diagram: archetype 7t Pi P2 (Y) P Y r 7 t\ B 0 Ab V Ib 0 K q(Fb) Gh The Supplementary Material The supplementary material (cf. above, p. 11) in Rev. VI, as it appears in Gh, consists of chapters 110-122 and of addiciones or declaraciones to chapters 3, 5, 9- 10, 15-20, 28, 30-32, 35-36, 39, 41, 52, 71, 75, 94-95, 98 and 102. In order to single out good MSS. for the edition of these texts, I compared the stemmata codicum used by Undhagen and myself for the edition of the supplementary material in Rev. I (p. 228) and Rev. VII (p. 112), respectively. MSS. common for both stemmata were chosen, viz. F K q; in both books, F and K belong to one branch, whereas q is the only representative of a second. In Undhagen's stemma there is also the MS. Ua, which alone represents one branch of the MS. tradition; as regards this important MS., see Rev. I, pp. 113 f., 218. For Rev. VII, Ua was not taken into account, simply because it does not contain any material supplementary to that book. It contains, as far as Rev. VI is concerned, on foll. 143r-160v, texts corresponding to addiciones and declaraciones to the chapters mentioned above with the exception of 32, 71, 94-95, 98 (half the text is missing) and 102 (ch. 110- 122 are not contained in the MS.). Undhagen's opinion (p. 218) that Ua holds a unique position among MSS. with supplementary material, made it natural to collate it for the present edition. In order to counterbalance F and K, representing the same branch both in Rev. V and Rev. VII, I also chose from my detailed stemma in Rev. VII (p. 76) a second representative of the q branch, viz. s. For the lacuna in q (cf. above, p. 17), I used Fb. There are reasons to believe that the relationship between these MSS. is very much the same as was established for Rev. I and Rev. VII, e.g. in so far as F K (and Gh) add igitur after 110:1 Quidam, whereas q and s omit habeant in § 6 of the same chapter. On the other hand, it seems to be difficult to relate Ua to any of the other MSS. There is an omission common to Ua and s (31:58-60), but since there are several q s errors not appearing in Ua, this Ua s error seems to be due to coincidence. Individual errors seldom appear in Ua, but there is one in the addition of qui before 35:47 audiens. The material, though meagre, would justify a stemma 
 
 
 
 
 19 codicum with three branches, represented by F K, Ua and q (Fb) s, respectively; Gh often goes with F and/or K. Not all the supplements, however, appear in all of these MSS. VI is that the texts are scattered in some of the MSS. and therefore sometimes difficult to locate. As 
 
 
 
 
 20 to the arrangement in F, I refer the reader to Hollman, pp. 30 ff. , 50 f.; the supplements in K are accounted for by Hollman, p. 47 n.1, and Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 107 f., and as regards 11a, see Hollman, p. 36 n. 1, and Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 114. It appears from the survey above that there is, in the Rev. MSS., no evidence at all for decl. 31:59-60, decl. 71, the very short decl. 95 and for 118:7-8: of the sources discussed so far, these texts are extant in Gh only. Decl. 95 is obviously a redactional note made by the Gh editors. In the present edition it has therefore been relegated to the apparatus, as was the case with a similar text in Rev. V (see p. 72 f. of that edition). Considerably more interesting are the other texts; as they contain allegedly Bridgettine material these are printed in the edition, although decl. 31:59-60 is very likely to have been taken over from A&P (cf. the critical apparatus). Dec!. 71 is in Gh said to have as its basis a letter from Birgitta's confessor- Petrus Olavi to Bishop Nicolaus Hermanni (1326-1391), and since the bishop is referred to as dead, the Gh text must have been given its form after 1391. There is, however, another and it would seem in some respects more reliable version of decl. 71 than the one in Gh; as a matter of fact, it may very weil have been the source of the Gh text. It is contained in the MS. A 64 of the Royal Library of Stockholm, which was published by K.G. Grandinson in 1888 (see `Bibliography'). The text corresponding to decl. 71 is in this MS. found on foll. 9r- 10r, and in the present edition it is printed (with some minor corrections in Grandinson's text) immediately after decl. 71. 
 
 
 
 
 Principles of Edition The Reconstruction of the Archetype It goes without saying that the principal source in establishing the Rev. VI text is the archetype of the MSS. used. The reconstruction of this archetype is based on a comparison between the four hyparchetypes, pi, (32 (=p), y and o (=n). A reading extant in all or any three of these is then most likely to have been in the archetype as weil. When there is one reading in two of the hyparchetypes and another in the two others (or, more seldom, when three readings are represented by the hypar- chetypes), the choice is of course more problematic and has be to be made with regard to contextual and linguistic arguments. In the cases where p has one reading and 't another and no contextual or linguistic facts seem to rule out either as erroneous, I have adopted for the present edition the same method as in my earlier editions, viz. the general preference of [3 to n; cf. Rev. V, p. 67, and Rev. VII, p. 94 (the same evaluation made by Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 225). Archetypal errors will be discussed in the following chapter `Textual Criticism'. Rev. VI Chapters Extant in Other Rev. Books Since Rev. VIII is secondary to Rev. VI, I did not collate the Rev. VIII MSS. for the establishment of the text of Rev. VI 26, 41, 82 and 95, i.e. the chapters common to these two books (cf. above, p. 11). On the other hand, to the extent doubts can arise as to the authenticity of the archetypal text in any of these Rev. VI chapters, there is reason to take the corresponding Rev. VIII text into consid- eration. This applies to one case only (82:1), and as a consequence regard will be paid to Rev. VIII in dealing with this passage in the chapter on textual criticism. There are many similarities between Rev. VI 19:1-19 and Rev. V reu. 8 (see Rev. V, p. 18 note 17). One passage from VI 19 will be discussed in the chapter on textual criticism; however, the Rev. V text does not contribute to the solution of the textual problem in question. Moreover, VI 17:11-15 gives roughly the same text as Ex. 57:1-7; no passage, however, from ch. 17 is obscure as regards the text of the archetype. The same is true of VI 111, the contents of which correspond to Ex. 61:4 and Ex. 96. Rev. VI 63 is on the whole identical with IV 136:1-9. The heading of the latter text includes some information which is lacking in VI 63:R: it dates the revelation to per duos annos ante iubileum, i.e. two years before the Holy Year 1350 (as regards this way of expressing difference of time, see my article `Zeitdifferenzbe- stimmungen in birgittinischen Texten', 1966), and it mentions Bishop Hemming 
 
 
 
 
 22 and Prior Petrus as bearers of the revelation in question to Pope Clement VI (cf. Klockars, Biskop Hemming av Åbo, p. 156 ff.). The most interesting difference in the text itself seems to be § 3 iudicans in VI as compared to audiens in IV, but it is hard to say which reading is primary. Both texts include in § 5 the phrase (nomen tuum) quod vocasti (in terris) for an expected quod habuisti, or the like. The construction is striking but may be regarded as a development of the phrase vocare nomen alcis, followed by a name, `call sb. sth.', which so often appears in the Vulgate (e.g. Matth. 1,25), and it is then natural to interpret the words as — sarcastically — referring to the name which the person in question — Pierre Roger — took as Pope, i.e. Clement, 'the name you gave yourself'. As ch. 63 does not include any other passage the archetypal reading of which is either incorrect or dubious, the text in IV 136 was accordingly left out of consideration. The Headings In addition to the headings which introduce the individual chapters, there are in some MSS. also lists of headings, normally put at the beginning of each book; cf. Rev. VII, p. 89. Of the Rev. VI MSS., such lists appear in P Y B 0 (=p) and Ib q v (=n). They are all placed in the beginning of Rev. VI with the exception of 0 (in which the list is found on fol. 186vb). The list headings were referred to above, p. 15, but there will be reason to return to them later, viz. in the chapter on textual criticism, when dealing with textual problems appearing in headings. Apart from this the list headings were left out of account; cf. Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 226. As far as the headings of chs. 110-122 are concerned, there is no proper MS. tradition: the headings are omitted from some MSS. and in the others they display great though contextually and linguistically insignificant differences. In this situa- tion I decided to print the Gh headings of these revelations and leave the others out of account; as for a parallel to this mode of procedure, see my edition of Rev. V, p. 69 f. Old Swedish Texts The relation between the Latin text of the Rev. and existing OS texts has in later years attracted new interest, as witnessed by works of Aili, Eklund, Moberg, Öberg and Wollin. In my edition of Rev. V (1971), I had reason to deal with the question of priority and could then make profitable use of the important OS Cod. Ups. C 61 (pp. 19-33; Wollin 1981, p. 6, is incorrect), and in an article from 1987 (TU problem i den heliga Birgittas uppenbarelser') I returned to the OS question in analyzing a passage from Rev. I. Reliable results of investigations in this field presuppose access to reliable texts. In the reconstruction of a Latin text for a meaningful comparative study, there may occasionally be reason to consult the OS text, primarily in those cases in which one may suspect that the archetype does not preserve the original reading. 
 
 
 
 
 23 In such cases the authentic Latin text can, as a matter of principle, be restored with the aid of the OS reading, viz. if the OS archetype did not depend on the Latin archetype (theoretically, OS readings can also be correct per coniecturam). From a purely theoretical point of view, Latin priority is in such cases nevertheless possible, the source of the OS text then being an exponent of the Latin tradition between the original and the corrupted archetype. However, since there is reason to believe that the OS version we know from Klemming's certainly defective edition in BU is based on a source which is considerably later than the Latin archetype (cf. Rev. VII, p. 104, Wollin 1981, p. 5 n. 6), the consequence seems to be that if an OS text is correct and the corresponding Latin archetypal text is corrupt, then the OS text was independent not only of the archetype of the Latin tradition but also of its original; the improbable alternative is that we are dealing with two different translations from the Latin. On the other hand, irrespective of priority or dependency, a correct OS reading cannot of course justify changes in a Latin text if this is preserved in its original form, represented by the primary codex of Alphonso's redaction. (It would appear that Aili, p. 76 n. 8, has taken up a similar position in this fundamental question.) To the extent, however, that mistranslations appear in the Latin redaction, these should be clarified by the editor, as Öberg (1986) rightly points out p. 71. In order to establish the possible use of OS texts in the reconstruction of the Latin Rev. VI, I took as a starting-point Wess6f s account in Svensk medeltid, II, Birgittatexter, pp. 73 ff., 95 ff. Here Wess&I lists texts, 'the OS versions of which are more original than the Latin ones' (p. 76). Among them there are also some Rev. VI chapters , viz. ch . 118 in Codex Bergman (c. 1420), Lund University Library, and chs. 28 (in part), 31 and 32 in MS. Skokloster 5 in 4:o (c. 1400), Riksarkivet, Stockholm, constituting, in this MS., part of the so-called 'Bridgettine Norse texts' (as to the inadequacy of this designation, see Wess&i, p. 88 f.). As far as ch. 118 in Codex Bergman is concerned, printed by Klemming in BU 4, the passage mina gärning. Hulka iak foreuiste af äuärdelico. Oc mik thäkkis at tee oc vppenbara h ä n n e (p. 125,1 ff.) supports the s Gh reading opus, quod ab eterno presciui et ostendere e i michi placet in § 6; the reading of the archetype seems to have been eam here (cf. the crit. app.). The 'Bridgettine Norse' texts, too, are printed in BU 4, and here a comparison between VI 28:5 and the corresponding passage on p. 497,13 has most interesting consequences for the punctuation in a number of Latin passages. The OS reading Swa ok ek. Ek löste etc. indicates through word-order and the repetition of the subject ek that 28:5 Sic ego is one separate clause, and for this reason I punctuate Sic ego; tanto etc. In consequence with this, the correct punctuation in 30:11 seems to be Sic dyabolus; si voluntas tua semper fuerit firma. . ., rodere quippe potest eam dyabolus. . ., sed etc., and the omission of the first dyabolus from Gh may very weil have been caused by a misunderstanding of the syntactic structure in question. The same is true of § 13 of the same chapter Sic tu; si voluntas tua fuerit etc.: here Gh omits tu. Interestingly enough, there is an example in § 29, too, Sic ego; si omnes 
 
 
 
 
 24 bonos . . . educerem etc. In this case, ego was not deleted by the Gh editors, and perhaps it was considered the subject of educerem. Their opinion may have been correct on this point, but I have nevertheless chosen a punctutation which brings the case into syntactic accordance with the others. This analysis demonstrates that a comparison with an OS text which is independ- ent of the Latin redaction, contributes to a better understanding of the latter, in this case to the preservation and elucidation of a feature typical of Birgitta's style (cf. also 33:1). Some passages from the other chapters of interest here, viz. 31 and 32, will be accounted for in the following chapter `Textual Criticism', since they (as distinct from 118:6 and 28:5) contain suspected archetypal errors. The use of seruire in 31:26 plus quam seruiuit, where meruit would have been expected, is probably to be explained as a translation error. In the 'Bridgettine Norse' texts (BU 4, p. 494,7), however, there is no literal counterpart to this phrase. As regards 32:9 extra se factus, see 'Glossarium' s. extra. A&P Yet another source to take into consideration in establishing the Latin text of Rev. VI is A&P. As appears from the critical apparatus, there are to decl. 3, 9, 30, 31 and 36 and to chs. 76, 78, 80-81, 97 and 107 parallel texts in A&P. Some passages taken from these chapters will be dealt with in the following chapter on textual criticism; in two of them, viz. 76:5 and 80:1, a comparison with corresponding A&P texts proved profitable. Cursus In my edition of Rev. VII, I found (p. 96 f.) that the frequency of cursus was considerably higher in those revelations which have the form of letters than in the others. The question of letters within the Rev. corpus was dealt with by Öberg (1969), and on p. 8 f. he correctly points out that VI 63 belongs to this group. I therefore found it necessary to establish whether the choice of readings in this chapter is affected if consideration is paid to cursus, but this in fact never seems to be the case, nor can I see that Rev. VI as a whole is characterized by any intended prose rhythm. An investigation of 537 cadences carried out in accordance with the method introduced by T. Janson, Prose Rhythm in Medieval Latin from the 9th to the 13th Century, Studia Latina Stockholmiensia, 20 (1975), thus resulted in practically identical figures for the observed frequency and the expected one (if cursus is not aimed at), as far as the cursus velox is concerned. Orthography In matters of orthography I follow the same principles as in my earlier editions (see Rev. V, p. 72), which means that the spelling has been normalized in accordance 
 
 
 
 
 25 with the usage norrrally observed in the MSS. Orthographical divergencies in the sources (including Gh) are very seldom listed in the apparatus. Of spellings not accounted for in Rev. V and VII it may be mentioned that the MSS. prefer auctoritas as compared to autoritas in Gh (e.g. 7:1 and 34:16), and for this reason I adopt auctoritatem also in the Gh version of 71:3 (which brings the spelling into accordance with the one in the parallel text in Cod. Holm. A 64). The Apparatuses Since the establishment of the text is based on a comparison between the four hyparchetypes f31, 132, y and o and not between the individual MSS. , the main objective of the critical apparatus is to give running information on these four sources. Individual readings are somtimes also given, but to a considerably lesser extent than was the case in my editions of Rev. V and Rev. VII: individual discrepancies from an otherwise unanimous MS. tradition have been listed only when considered to be of interest from the point of view of palaeography, textual criticism (correct or noteworthy emendations) or language in general. It is thus not possible to judge from the apparatus the quality of individual MSS. as reflected in the number of individual errors. On the other hand, I deemed it appropriate to account for all Gh readings, since they give immediate information on the differ- ences between the present edition and previous texts, both Gh itself and texts based on that edition (Lunckn's Swedish translation and other Latin editions, cf. Hollman, p. 26 f.), the letters in the margin of the present edition refer to the paragraphs into which the Gh text is divided. The critical apparatus informs of readings diverging from the printed text, but now and then the latter too is given in the apparatus, for the sake of clarity. It also includes references and parallels, but only if they are considered to justify the printed text or elucidate a certain reading. Other references have been brought together in a separate apparatus. 
 
 
 
 
 Textual Criticism In this chapter, a number of cases will be discussed in which the archetypal reading, reconstructed according to the principles put forward in the preceding chapter, for various reasons gave rise to suspicions as regards its authenticity. In the vast majority of the cases discussed, the archetype was deemed to diverge from the original wording, and the conclusion must be that the archetype was not possibly identical with the original; my investigation into the Rev. V and Rev. VII MS. traditions yielded the same results (p. 74 ff. and pp. 8, 100 ff., respectively). It may added that textual problems are also commented upon in the chapter entitled `The Manuscripts' and, in the form of quoted parallels, in the apparatus criticus. 7:8 Inde, quemadmodum corpus mortuum cadit in t e r r am (y Gh terra ceteri), sic anime eorum descendent in infernum etc. In all probability, the reading of the archetype was in terra, and considering the Medieval Latin usage of, for instance, venire in followed by the ablative (or ibi), terra cannot be ruled out as an impossible reading from the beginning. However, a systematic study of cadere in the Rev. and, in particular, cadere followed by in terralm, demonstrates that the accusative is regularly chosen, e.g. I 15:13 corpus ipsius cadit per mortem in terram, I 48:28 de corpore hominum, quod cadit in terram (terra in two MSS.), IV 54:2 femina. . . extrahit. . .totum corpus, usquequo cadit in terram and VII 15:25 mater eius. . .volens cadere in terram, whereas cadere in terra lacks parallels. On these grounds, I regard terra in our case as an archetypal error, a parallel to which is given by two MSS. in I 48:28. The error is in the edition corrected in accordance with the y Gh reading. 8:9 iudicabo eos gladio meo, qui omnia membra eorum a capite vsque ad pedes con c i d e t (scripsi conscindet Ab conscideret K conscidet ceteri Gh). The stem conscid-, which was in the archetypal reading, does not have any parallel in the Rev. corpus, but the same is true of both concid- and conscind-. There is a case of the perfect participle conscissi, Ex. 83:1 terra contremuit et lapides conscissi (scissi F) sunt, apparently coined after Matth. 27,51 et terra mota est et petrae scissae sunt. However, the presence of the word gladio makes our case comparable to Ezech. 5,2 concides gladio and particularly 23,25 nasum tuum et aures tuas praecident et quae remanserint gladio concident; there is thus good reason to believe that 8:9 is an example not of conscindere but of concidere. The problem is then whether the spelling conscid- for concid- is to be accepted as authentic. Arguments can be advanced in favour of this, such as the spelling 
 
 
 
 
 27 conscilium for concilium, attested in SG1 (1, p. 207). This kind of misspelling is in reality rather common in Medieval Latin MSS.; as to further examples and further literature, see Rev. V, p. 51 n. 35. In order to account for the situation in the Rev., I studied cases of cid- and els- with other prefixes than con- and found that there is, in fact, some support for the assumption that the spelling ci- was generally observed by the archetype in such cases. In Rev. V int. 10:5 circumscidi occurs in A13 , but all other MSS. have circumcidi, and in § 29 of the same chapter, Ab alone has circumscisus against circumcisus in the others; in § 37, all MSS. have circumci- sio, including Ab. In I 32:16 the reading inscidi appears in one MS., but all the others have incidi; the same MS. has for I 34:16 incidi the erroneous inscindi. In I 58:1 the 131 group (except Y1) has inscidi, but Undhagen rightly follows the other MSS. in printing incidi. There are also several examples of precid- and precis- (e.g. I 17:4 Precident with the variant readings prescindent and prescident, VI 52:29 precisa with the variant reading prescisa), and this makes me inclined to believe that the correct reading of VI 52:73 is precidens (supported by precisa in the following paragraph, with prescisa as a variant reading): it is, however, very probable that the archetype had the reading prescindens here (cf. the crit. app.). It should be added that the verb abscido was for obvious reasons excluded from this investigation, but it is nevertheless of interest in this connexion in so far as an incorrect morphemic analysis may have contributed to the spellings scid- and scis- in other verbs. To sum up, a number of passages exist in which the archetype seems to have had the stems -cid- and -cis-, respectively, spellings that have been distorted in the MS. tradition. Using this material, I suspect that conscidet in 8:9 is an archetypal error of the same kind, the distortion of an original concidet. 10:5 Primo saluabitur ab igne, quem patitur pro luxuria, si aliquis vellet secundum tres ordines Ecclesie, c o n i u g a t o r u m (coniugatarum B Ab) scilicet et viduarum et virginum, vnam feminam tradere nuptui pro anima eius, aliam reli- gioni, terciam, que posset stare in viduitate etc. The reading of the archetype must have been coniugatorum, whereas coniuga- tarum was introduced either in the (32 hyparchetype or independently in B and Ab, in any case probably due to viduarum and virginum and the following text in § 5, referring to women (virgo can in itself, as is well known, refer to men). Does this alteration in B Ab, then, correspond to the original wording? There are some passages in the Rev. which might support it. The heading of IV 53 speaks of omnem viduam et omnem virginem et omnem coniugatam, but here the feminine gender is necessary since coniugatam refers to § 1 mater (in § 6, the tres vite are described as virginitas, viduitas et coniugium, just as is the case in Rev. ex. 20:1). The heading of IV 71 mentions tres laudabiles status, scilicet statum virginitatis, coniugalis et vidualis, and a counterpart to coniugalis in the following text is a coniugata twice compared to a virgo (§ 14). But here, too, the feminine form is natural since the text deals with Birgitta's daughter. In other texts we find the 
 
 
 
 
 28 masculine form. Birgerus Gregorii, Officium Sancte Birgitte (ed. Undhagen), leccio 1:2 (p. 185 f.) speaks of Ecclesia, que. . .filios. . .tribus ordinibus seu statibus, virginum scilicet, continencium et coniugatorum. . .pulchrificat et decorat, and in § 9 he mentions Non . . .solum virgines et continentes, verum eciam coniugati. As a matter of fact, when the tres ordines are quoted as a more or less stereotype formula, then coniugati is used. The function is then neutral as to gender, the masculine form including the feminine sex (whereas the feminine form always excludes the masculine sex). This is the case with Thomas Aquinas, Summa, Suppl. q. 96, a. 2 fructus (sc.dividitur). . .in fructum coniugatorum, viduarum et virginum, a. 4 coniugatorum. . .virginum. . .viduarum. As far as I can see, 10:5 may contain an example of this stereotype phrase, and on these grounds I retain the archetypal reading coniugatorum as authentic. 10:6-7 Secundo, quia in gula tripliciter peccauit.. . ., (7) et ideo, qui voluerit, c o 1- liga t (scripsi colligere codd. Gh) in honore Dei. . .propter hanc triplicem gulam tres pauperes. . ., et ministret (ministrare Y r Ab ministraret P B 0) eis etc. The reading of the archetype, quia. . ., et ideo qui voluerit colligere etc., is problematic, primarily in so far as there is no main clause. The analysis of this passage may start with colligere. It is evident that this infinitive is syntactically motivated only if it is subordinate to voluerit. However, the syntactic pattern of § 9 debet, qui voluerit, colligere septem pauperes. . .et lauet pedes ipsorum etc., § 15 qui voluerit, vadat etc. and § 19 colligat, qui voluerit, omnes pauperes etc. supports the suspicion that colligere, if authentic, is not subordinate to voluerit and that voluerit stands alone without any infinitive. It is tempting to solve the problem by changing et into <deb>et (cf. § 9), which restores the missing apodosis and removes the unwarranted et. This solution, implying a co-ordination of debet, followed by the infinitive, and a subjunctive (ministret), gives an exact parallel to the syntax in § 9 (et lauet). The problem is that the syntactic structure presupposed, i.e. causal clause and apodosis introduced by the finite verb followed by ideo, is unparallelled in the Rev. (and maybe in other texts). Moreover, ideo of the kind relevant here — i.e. ideo preceded by a causal expression, not ideo followed by a causal or final clause, e.g. I 58:14 Cui ideo dedi libertatem, ut seruiret michi — is in the Rev. either the first word in the clause or the second, but in the latter case the preceding word is nearly always et. In the very comprehensive material there are only two exceptions to this rule, and in both cases non is involved: III 22:1 Non est ideo iudicandus (with another meaning than Ideo non est iudicandus) and VI 65:88 licet pars Marie optima est, non tamen ideo pars Marthe mala est. To sum up: an alteration of the text to <deb>et ideo would introduce an unparallelled syntax, whereas et ideo, though strange here, is an extremely weil attested combination in the Rev. In this situation, I preferred to regard colligere as an archetypal error influenced by the preceding voluerit, which was mechanically taken to require an infinitive. The original reading may then have been colligat, which gives a normal co-ordination with the following ministret. This ministret was certainly the reading 
 
 
 
 
 29 of the archetype, and ministrare (an alteration made independently in Y r and Ab) reveals an effort to normalize the syntax. In the edition, I retain the unnecessary et (before ideo) and consider it apodotic, a usage which is not without parallels in the Rev.: see, for instance, VII 17:R and 29:13. Interestingly enough, the OS text, as given in BU 3, p. 16,14, is hulkin som vil taki, and the subjunctive taki is a perfect counterpart to colligat. However, the reading taki was introduced within the OS tradition, as indicated by the important Cod. Ups. C 61, which has hwilkin som vill tagha (p. 671,15). 10:8 Insuper ministret illis tribus vestes sufficienter et lectisternia, prout eis c o mp e- t e r e (K expetere ceteri Gh) et expedire viderit. Whereas the syntagm eis. . .expedire viderit does not involve any linguistic or contextual difficulties — cf. , for instance, Rev. IV 83:8 viuit. . .prout expedit corpori and Ex. 85:7 prout nature congruit et expedit — it is hard to understand how the archetypal eis expetere is to be explained here. The verb expetere is admittedly not altogether frequent in the Rev., but there is one clear example in VII 28:1 Alii vero expetunt ab eisdem maledictis maleficis de presciencia futurorum, with the unprob- lematic meaning 'ask for', 'request', not applicable to our case. Another example (and with the same meaning) occurs in VI 35:R (see below, p. 39), but there the archetypal reading was expectabatur, and expetebatur is an emendation in G. The intransitive use of expetere known from Ancient Latin with the meaning `befall' (cf. Thes. 5:2,1692,15 ff.) lacks parallels not only in the Rev. but also, it would seem, in Medieval Latin dictionaries as weil. Nor is the intransitive meaning of expetere very appropriate here. Considering the attested usage of this verb in the Rev., something like prout naturam expetere et eis expedire viderit would have appeared unproblematic in the context (cf. Ex. 85:7, quoted above). However, in order to minimize changes in the transmitted text, I preferred to replace expetere by the K reading competere. This verb appears now and then in the Rev. (cf. Hollman, p. 237), in the same impersonal function as suits our case, e.g. I 16:10 Propterea quia filius meus est sponsus anime et est dominus super te (sc. diabole), ideo competit tibi fugere coram eo. The meaning of competere then comes very close to that of expedire, but the resulting co-ordination of synonyms seems to be a stylistic feature characteristic of the Rev.: cf. e.g. I 30:9 lectus quiescendi seu quietudinis, II 14:52 consideracio temporis. . .transacti et preteriti, VII 20:26 se corrigere et. . . emendare, Ex. 80:9 regere et gubernare; cf. Hofmann-Szantyr, p. 785 ff. An original competere in our case, restored per coniecturam by the K copyist, may have been changed to expetere due to the influence of the prefix in the following co-ordinated infinitive. 10:27 Pro septimo, scilicet quod iudex fuit et iudicium suum commisit vicariis iniquis, propter quod (quot Ib ) est in manibus demonum. If propter quod is meant to introduce a subordinate clause, this gives an abnormal syntax with a missing apodosis. It is not altogether impossible that some 
 
 
 
 
 30 finite verb is to be understood after Pro septimo, e.g. peccauit, but I am more inclined to believe that quod has here the function of a demonstrative pronoun and that propter quod with the same meaning as conclusive adverbs such as propterea or ideo sums up the content of the preceding causal clause; in this respect it is comparable to 10:7 ideo discussed above. This phenomenon, i.e. a relative word introducing the apodosis, is known from Late Latin, as appears from Hofmann- Szantyr, p. 570 e (with further literature; cf. also B. Löfstedt in Orpheus. Rivista di umanitd classica e cristiana, 11,1990, p. 336). In this interpretation, propter quod is an example of the extensive use in Medieval Latin of relative pronouns and adverbs in demonstrative function. A good example of this is given by Propter quam voluntatem in § 21 of the same chapter. Striking examples are found in Ex. 8:3-4 habuit multas reuelaciones de statu vrbis, in quibus arguebat Dominus noster Ihesus Christus excessus et peccata inhabitancium vrbem cum graui comminacione vindicte. (4) Q u e reuelaciones etc.: here not only the repetition of reuelaciones but also the distance between the two words indicates that Que does not have a relative but a demonstrative function. Other instructive examples of the demon- strative relative are found in 39:1, 39:8, 46:11 and 73:3 of the present edition. As regards this usage in other Medieval Latin texts, I would like to call attention to cases like Carpini, Ystoria Mongalorum (ed. Wyngaert), p. 94 Qui cuyccan predic- tus and, still more striking, a papal letter from 1438 published by Tore Nyberg in Dokumente und Untersuchungen zur inneren Geschichte der drei Birgittenklöster Bayerns 1420-1570, I, p. 87, et quequidem abbatissa: here the presence of et excludes any relative interpretation of que. 10:32 dicatur vna missa de qualibet sollempnitate filii mei, scilicet vna de natiuita- te. . ..et vna de pentecosten (penthecoste o Gh). The archetypal pentecosten seems remarkable, and one may ask whether the original form is not restored in the o Gh reading. A number of parallels indicate, however, that the stereotyped accusative pentecosten has in fact been in use: DS I, p. 8 (a. 846) in Pascha, in Pentecosten, in natali Domini, Rimbertus, Vita Anskarii (ed. Waitz), p. 21 adveniente die sancto pentecosten, AC I, p. 358 (a. 1338) a proximo festo pentecosten, DS VIII, p. 63 (a. 1361) in vigilia pentecosten. There is thus good reason to consider the form pentecosten authentic in our case. There is another example in Rev. VI, viz. 86:1: Quidam sacerdos in die p en t e c o s ten ((31 0 y penthecostes ceteri Gh) celebrauit etc. Since pentecosten is represented here in three of the four branches of the MS. tradition, it seems necessary to assume that this was the reading of the archetype as well. Considering the fact that pentecoste and parasceue are in many respects compa- rable, it must be admitted that there is some probability that the reading paras- ceuen in 7:4 is correct (cf. the apparatus criticus). I nevertheless took it to be erroneous, since it differs from pentecosten in 10:32 and 86:1 not only in so far as it lacks parallels, but also in that its MS. support is weaker. 
 
 
 
 
 31 12:1-3 Beata virgo loquebatur ad sponsam dicens: "Sponsa filii mei, tu salutabas me et comparabas alueario apum. Ego certe f u i (scripsi sic fui K fui apiarium Gh fui apis ceteri). Nam corpus meum fuit quasi lignum... (3) Hoc lignum factum est aluearium, quando illa benedicta apis, filius Dei etc. The imagery used by Birgitta in this chapter does not lack parallels, as appears, for example, from 44:5 Apis enim ista ego sum, omnium creator et Dominus, qui de apiario tunc exiui, cum fornram humanam assumens apparui in ea visibilis. Among the MSS. it was observed only in K that 12:1 apis, used of Mary, is impossible. That the transmitted text is unacceptable was also seen by the Gh editors, and from a palaeographic point of view apiarium is certainly attractive. Theoretically, the archetypal apis could be retained, if a negation has been lost, but such a solution does not appear stylistically convincing. My way of solving the problem, i.e. the deletion of apis, was partly influenced by the K reading, partly by the observation that certe in the Rev. appears now and then in very short, sometimes even incomplete clauses, e.g. VI 85:4 Quid credunt homines. . .? Numquid quod intrabunt in requiem meam? Certe non magis quam Lucifer, and VIII 48:155 0 o, quid habet modo rex de omnibus diuiciis suis? Certe non nisi damnum. Quid vero de honore? Certe non nisi pudorem. 13:3 sedes vero tunc, quando mentem tuam iugiter ad celestia eleuas considerando, qualis glo ria san c to r u m (sit gloria sanctorum Y2 Gh gloria sanctorum est r gracia sanctorum Ab est gloria sanctorum F2 gloria est sanctorum K) et vita sempiterna. Obviously, the qualis clause did not contain any finite verb in the archetype (as is also the case in Y1 and F1) and this has invited alterations of various kinds. The question is thus whether an ellipsis of this sort is nevertheless consistent with linguistic norms observed in the Rev. Elliptical forms of esse are comparatively common, e.g. I 1:10 Requiescas in brachiis deitatis mee, ubi nulla voluptas carnis sed gaudium et delectacio spiritus, 10:8 Sensi odorem, sed non qualis herbarum vel aliquid tale, VI 19:8 Quis compulit eum aut que inde vtilitas? , 31:11 Manus istius quasi grifi. Examples of indirect interrogative clauses, as in the case in question, are however more rare, but such passages do occur, e.g. VI 59:5 cepit cogitacio quedam pulsare animum meum,. . quomodo concepi aut quis pater filii nascituri; this ellipsis offended the corrector of F", who added esset. A better parallel to our case is found in Ex. 83:4, Vidit quoque in scriptura, quomodo viueret, qualis via et institucio mea et sanctorum meorum, et cogitauit secum etc. This ellipsis, too, has invited one correction, viz. in Gh which has the reading qualis erat. (It may, however, be noted that the elliptical clause in the last two cases is co-ordinated with a preceding normal indirect interrogative clause.) A reasonable conclusion to be drawn from this is that ellipsis in indirect interrogative clauses was more easily tolerated in the Rev. original than by some readers in later times. On these grounds, I consider the archetypal reading in 13:3 authentic. Other cases of ellipsis will be discussed below, p. 47 f. (65:22). 
 
 
 
 
 32 13:4 debes disposita esse sicut virgo d e s p o n s a tura (desponsata r Ab desponsanda F2 K Gh). The archetypal reading desponsatura was thus changed either to desponsata or desponsanda, and typical of these latter readings is that the virgo is the object of the action implied. This also corresponds to the normal usage in the Rev., e.g. VI 33:1 Tu es similis sponso, qui desponsauit sibi sponsam etc. and Ex. 70:1 pauper . . .disponebat facere nupcias filie sue desponsate, but there is one passage in which the girl is the agent. In II 21:16 there is a virgo cuidam desponsata, i.e. correspond- ing to normal usage, but in the following paragraph the same girl receives the following recommendation (21:17): Consulo tibi, ne credas ei, quem desponsasti. This means, by the way, that the information given both by SG1 (s.v.) and Undhagen, Rev. I, p. 451 is incomplete in so far as desponsare is said there to mean `take to w i f e'. The actual usage of desponsare in the Rev., admitting of the woman as the active subject, supports the archetypal reading of 13:4, desponsatura then being used intransitively. An alternative interpretation, in my opinion less probable, is to consider desponsatura an example of the rare usage of the future participle used in passive function (cf. Hofmann-Szantyr, p. 289 f.). There is however, to the best of my knowledge, no certain example of this in the Rev. corpus (but one uncertain example in RS, see Eklund, p. 79). 19:1 Hec vox est in auribus meis quasi vox de fistula et quasi son u s (F2 q Gh uox Abl uox sonus ceteri) canne. Vox labiorum eius est quasi sonus (uox sonus r) dunrum lapidum collisorum. Obviously, the archetype had uox before the first sonus. The veord does not have any intelligible function in the tontext, and I suspect it was erroneously added due to influence from the preceding quasi vox. The r reading later in the paragraph demonstrates how easily such an error is made. 23:18 Ergo, fili mi dukissime, propter bonitatem tuam et preces meas, que te in v t e- r o (pi y vtero meo (32 o Gh) portaui, miserere ei! Irrespective of which reading was in the archetype, vtero or vtero meo, the distribution of the readings implies at least two changes in the MS. tradition, made independently. My assumption that meo was more easily added than deleted is supported by Rev. IV 104:1 Scribitur, quod ego dicebar benedicta, que te portaui in utero, which is the archetypal reading, but meo was added in some MSS. This case comes very close to ours: the two relative clauses differ only in word-order. We have thus all reason to believe that, in 23:18, vtero (without meo) was the reading not only of the archetype but of the original as weil. Other supporting parallels are I 10:12 Cumque haberem eum in utero and VI 19:21 Ego portaui eum quasi in vtero cum magna difficultate. There are certainly cases of an authentic in vtero meo, too, e.g. I 42:9 Ego enim habui filium in utero meo cum deitate — where no MS. omits meo, cf. above. The material indicates that both alternatives corresponded to accepted usage. 
 
 
 
 
 33 27:10 Ego permisi eis moderate habere honores, habere eciam diuicias (scripsi amicos rodd. Gh) et habere voluptatem moderate. The following paragraphs, 11-13, contain a continuation and development of what is said in § 10; Christ declares how man should behave in regard to honor (§ 11), diuicie (§ 12) and voluptas (§ 13). Man's actual conduct in these respects is accounted for in § 14 with the same consecutive order, in honore. . .de diuiciis. . .in voluptate. Thus, the archetypal amicos does not have any counterpart in the following text, the repeated diuicie of which does not have any counterpart in § 10, and the inevitable conclusion is that § 10 amicos must be an archetypal error for diuicias. From a palaeographic point of view, this confusion is not so remarkable as it might appear: cf. Cappelli, pp. 12 and 97 (abbrevations of amicis and diviciae, respectively). It is curious that this error passed unnoticed in the MSS. The same is true of both the OS translation (BU 2,55,4 f. Jak vnte thom matelica haua värlz hedhir ok vini ok lusta) and Lun&n's (3, p. 62 jag tillät dem att på måttfullt vis hava ära, vänner och nöjen'). 30:8 Hic (sc. dyabolus) radicem voluntatis, si (non post si add. r2 K) fuerit <i n>- s ta b i l i s (0 F2 q Gh stabilis ceteri) ad paciendum, morsu suo findit etc. The reading of the archetype, stabilis, does not make sense, as appears from the heading, si vero instabilis (sc. voluntas est), tunc roditur a talpa dyabolo etc. The error was sometimes observed in the tradition of the text and therefore corrected, though in two different ways, viz. through the addition of non and in-, respective- ly. It is not easy to make a choice between these alternatives (si non is unproble- matic in itself, as appears e.g. from 10:23, 17:8, 24:10, 33:14 etc.). Both restore the meaning of the passage, and there are parallels also to the r2 K reading in so far as non. . .stabilis appears at some places, e.g. IV 107:23 Ex istis probatur, quod fides sua non est stabilis. It may, however, be argued that instabilis in the heading gives some support to the emendation instabilis; cf. also instabiles in § 17. Moreover, from a palaeographic point of view it may perhaps be maintained that in- written as i with a nasal stroke can more easily pass unnoticed than even an abbreviated non. 30:35 ille est verus frater,. . ., qui me Deum factum c a r n e (F K carnem Gh) fratrem suum agnoscit etc. It is reasonable to assume that the archetypal reading here was carne. A passage like Rev. V reu. 4:9 ego Deus factus sum in te caro might support the Gh emendation carnem — carne then being an archetypal error — but I nevertheless prefer to retain the MSS. reading as authentic with regard to a parallel in Ven. Fort. 2,2,10-12 (ed. Leo, p. 28), Quando venit ergo sacri plenitudo temporis, I missus est ab arce patris natus orbis conditor I atque ventre virginali ca r n e factus prodiit; cf. S. Blomgren, Studia Fortunatiana (1933), p. 112 n. 1, and Hofmann- Szantyr, p. 106 ff. 3 — Birger Bergh 
 
 
 
 
 34 31:10 Nos habemus ora aperta, quia s i c<u t> (P2 Ab v Gh sic P1 Y r B 0 Ib o) ipsa os suum apertum habuit ad omnia mundi suauia sed clausum ad te et ad honorem tuum, sic et ego habeo os meum apertum etc. Both readings, sic and sicut, are represented in all branches of the stemma, but the MS. support for sic is somewhat stronger. As being the lectio difficilior, this was most probably the archetypal reading as weil. It breaks, however, a syntactic pattern weil established in the Rev., illustrated by cases like I 20:11 Dico tibi pro certo, quod sicut Zacharias et Elizabeth. . .gaudebant. . ., sic et tu gaudebis, IV 18:14 Sicut enim Petrus. . .permansit. . ., sic et fides sancte Ecclesie. . .permanebit, 104:19 sicut omnis, qui pulsat, extra hostium est, sic et isti reges extra hostium sunt, VI 108:2 sicut ille predicabat infidelibus, sic et tu predicabas Iudeis. Obviously, ipsa os suum etc. is more appropriate as the protasis of the following sic et clause than — if the archetype were correct — as an explanation of the clause Nos habemus. In the 'Bridgettine Norse' version of this text (cf. above, p. 23), there is no counterpart at all to either sicut or sic, which indicates that the word was added in the Latin translation. Strangely enough, the word is missing in other OS texts, too, not only in BU 3, p. 67,3 but also in the above-mentioned Cod. Ups. C 61, p. 753,18, both representing the re-translation from Latin. To my opinion, this indicates that the Latin exemplar used had the syntactically bewildering sic, which was therefore so much more easily dropped. 31:13 Implebatur enim (sc. venter) et non sa cia b a tur (v Gh saciabitur Ib saciebatur ceteri). A parallel to the archetypal saciebatur is found in 35:18, where P2 v F have saciebatur, whereas (31 Ib q K Gh have saciabatur; the latter reading has thus somewhat stronger MS. support, but this of course does not prove that it was in the archetype as well. There are other examples of this kind of anomaly. In 35:13, the reading attediabatur is found in Y r B q K Gh and attediebatur in P 0 Ab y F. Two further cases, 39:23 anxiabatur (P2 B K Gh anxiebatur Pi Y 0 Ab y F q ansiebatur r) and 59:8 anxiabatur (P2 Y K Gh anxiebatur P1 r 132 Fb F), demonstrate that the morphological problem in question is connected to the imperfect passive indicative of first conjugation verbs ending in -iare. It would seem that the reading with -e- instead of -a- in these cases presupposes an accentuated -ba-: so for instance, in 62:1 all MSS. have anxiarer, and the same is true of I 15:28 attediantur and V int. 11:22 saciarentur. As regards other, comparable verbs in the Rev., cases like I 38:9 inebriabuntur (all MSS.) and VI 25:2 abbreviabuntur (breuiabuntur v) confirm the impression that the -e- variant only appears when followed by -ba-, as if it were a fourth conjugation verb like audiebatur. A further example of -eba- instead of an expected -aba- is given by Ex. 19:5: Hollman here prints contrariabantur, but two of the five sources used have contrariebantur. It is most probable that the archetypal reading of 31:13 was saciebatur, and in 39:23 and 59:8 the archetype may very weil have had anxiebatur; also in 35:13 and 35:18 the -eba- form may derive from the archetype. The question is then whether 
 
 
 
 
 35 this sort of morphological deviation from the norm could have appeared in the original as weil. MW (s.v.) lists the form anxiebatur (in a document from a. 1144), so the phenomenon is not limited to the Rev. tradition. On the other hand, it is noteworthy that some verbs in the Rev. ending in -iare are remarkably resistant to this sort of vowel change. The verb regraciari, for instance, appears VI 14:4 regraciabaris, 66:38 and Ex. 71:4 regraciabatur; in the first case there is only one variant reading, regraatiebaris (sic) in Ab, in the second there is regraciebatur in 0 F, but in Ex. 71 all (four) sources adhere to the regular form. Another example is humiliare: in RS § 27 humiliabantur is unanimously handed down in the MSS. (Eklund, pp. 103 and 178). Considering this and the fact that in all cases of-eba- discussed, at least some copyists have taken offence at the transmitted -eba- form, we have reason to believe that the -aba- form was in fact considered the correct one, and though the original may have deviated in one or more of the passages under discussion from what was in all probability regarded as the norm, I never- theless chose the alternative of bringing these cases into accordance with that norm. 31:29 Numquid non in lege tua scriptum est, quod, vbi vna voluntas et v nus <c on > s e n s u s (Y r2 Gh vnus sensus ceteri) matrimonialis fuerit, ibi coniunccio legalis fieri potest? The archetypal reading was evidently vnus sensus, but I cannot find any parallel to the expression sensus matrimonialis then implied, nor can I understand what sensus could possibly mean here. Since there is nothing in the Vulgate to corre- spond to the text, it seems legitimate to assume that the words in lege tua refer to canonical law. This is also the opinion of Klockars (1966), who on p. 154 refers to Decretales IV 1,23 and 4,5. This reference may be justified, but it is a fact that the passages referred to do not have any good equivalent in Birgitta's text (Corpus iuris canonici, ed. Friedberg, 2, pp. 670 and 682). Apparently, Birgitta does not quote directly but, using her own words, she accounts for a fundamental idea expressed in canonical law. In the Decretales passages referred to above, there are several examples of the word consensus, albeit never with matrimonialis but with other adjuncts such as legitimus and mutuns. If consensus is the correct reading — and I think it is — then vnus may appear a little disturbing. In the case of voluntas, vna is necessary, but as an attribute of consensus, a word with the meaning `one' seems uncalled for: consensus would in itself be enough and that might justify a suspicion that vnus in the MSS. corre- sponds to the lacking con-. A comparison with the 'Bridgettine Norse' text (cf. above, p. 23) nevertheless makes me inclined to believe that the Y r2 Gh reading vnus consensus restores, in fact, the original wording. The corresponding passage in this OS text (BU 4, p. 494,16) is et ia ok en vilia, and since the word consensus reasonably corresponds to ia (which could hardly have been rendered with sensus by the Latin translator), it is natural to ask for a Latin equivalent to et, and that is vnus. In this interpretation, con- was dropped in the archetype. The difference in 
 
 
 
 
 36 word-order between the Latin and the OS text is, I think, of minor importance in this context. 31:41 tantum eam dilexisti, vt te ipsum Deum pro ipsa t e (del. q om. K Gh) dares. The archetypal reading te. . .te implies a striking pleonasm, and it is justifiable to ask whether it really corresponds to the original wording. The 'Bridgettine Norse' text (BU 4, p. 495,7) does not have anything similar, but the text there is different also in so far as it lacks a counterpart to Deum. However, pleonasms of different kinds appear here and there in the Rev. corpus, and a passage demonstrating the pleonastic use of pronouns, as in our case, is Prol. Math. § 16: In quibus omnibus non suam sed Dei gloriam querens, latere quidem ob humilitatis custodiam maluis- set, nisi quantum ad proximorum salutem s e per imperium spiritus, vel pocius Christi, in spiritu sibi apparentis, certis s e personis manifestare iussa est. As to other examples, cf. I 18:2 Quia ill i, qui michi nunc domos edificant, ip s i sunt similes magistris edificantibus and VII 27:2 caue, quod e a, que modo audies,. . non loquaris e a etc. As regards the question of pleonastic repetition of pronouns, cf. also B. Löfstedt in Glotta 54,1976, p. 140 (with literature). The pleonastic use of conjunctions will be discussed below, p. 40 f. 32:10 discit (sc. serpens) pungere lapides et ligna et queque (quecumque P Ab) forcia. It is true that there are no parallels in the Rev. to plural forms of quisque whereas quecumque on the other hand occasionally appears in clauses without any finite verb and with the meaning 'all' or 'of any kind', e.g. III 9:3 audax ad quecumque virilia opera efficietur and VII 5:33 palacia, castellla, predia. . .atque alia quecumque multarum pecuniarum lucrativa. The P Ab reading, whether an intentional alteration or not, is thus in accordance with attested usage. However, it is not impossible to find examples of the plural of quisque in other Medieval Latin texts. Petrus Damiani (1007-1072) writes in his Rhytmus de gaudio Paradisi, 22, Nam et sancti quique velut sol praeclarus rutilant (ed. Lokrantz, p. 81), and Rodulfus Glaber, from the same century as Petrus Damiani, quite often uses this plural form, as pointed out by B. Löfstedt in Aevum. Rassegna di scienze storiche, linguistiche e filologiche 64,1990, p. 200. In A&P, pp. 47, 48, 50 and 51, we find the text eciam infirma huius mundi dignetur eligere, vt forcia queque confundat, in all four cases in letters from Sweden to the Pope, and it is therefore probably more than coincidence that almost exactly the same phrase also appears in Breviarium Lincopense (ed. Peters), p. 575, infirma mundi eligis vt fortia queque confundas. The ultimate source must be 1 Cor. 1,27 infirma mundi elegit Deus, ut confundat fortia. It seems reasonable to assume that the presence of the word forcia in our case has something to do with forcia queque in the texts mentioned, and consequently I regard queque as the original reading. It may be added that the 'Bridgettine Norse' version of this text (cf. above, p. 23) is of no interest here. 
 
 
 
 
 37 32:19 Docet eciam mater natum serpere et precedendo ducit ad feruorem solis, quando v i u e n do (K Gh seuiendo ceteri) superbe et dissolute incitat eum ad consimilia. There is no question about the archetypal reading here, which must have been seuiendo. Apart from the fact that the verb s(a)euire is otherwise unattested in the Rev., it is with regard to its meaning not very convincing here, whereas the K Gh emendation gives an unproblematic text. Since the 'Bridgettine Norse' version of ch. 32 does not depend on the Latin redaction (cf. above, p. 23), it is of course essential to study the corresponding reading of that text. According to BU 4, p. 485, 26 it has tha hon 1 if u i r siälf högfärdelika och lustelika. This makes it legitimate to assume that the correction in K Gh, viuendo, restores the original wording. Besides, the actual use of the adverbs superbe and dissolute in the Rev. confirms this. The latter appears in two other passages, IV 40:6 and VII 10:4, both times with a form of viuere. The former occurs with the verb spolire in IV 142:5 but with viuere in VI 52:R, exempla viuendi curiose et superbe. The erroneous reading seuiendo was probably conditioned by palaeographic resemblance to viuendo, and thus arose within the Latin tradition rather than as a translational error. The passage 32:19 provides, then, a further example of the importance of paying consideration to independent OS texts when reconstructing the original Latin text. 32:30 Nec filii eius prosperabuntur nec facient semen, nec memoria eorum in g e n e- r a c i o n e<m> (scripsi generacione K Gh) et generacionem erit. Only two sources were used for the edition of 32:30, K and Gh, but since they have the same reading, one may suspect that this was the archetypal reading as weil. In all probability, the text echoes Exod. 3,15 hoc nomen mihi est in aeternum, et hoc memoriale meum in generacionem et generacionem; in the edition it has been corrected accordingly. 34:22 Nunc ego rogo te, vt tu vadas nuncius meus ad summum caput Ecclesie, quia eciam si sederit ei (sedit ei v sederit ibi F Gh) Lucifer, verba filii mei secundum eius voluntatem perficientur. There are in the Rev. three occurrences of the phrase caput Ecclesie, the two others being I 41:9 and 52:11, and in all of these cases the expression refers to one and the same person, viz. Pope Clement VI (1342-1352); see Undhagen, Rev. I, pp. 361, 408, Klockars, Biskop Hemming av Åbo, p. 155 f. (cf. Sun&n, p. 71 f.). In I 41:17, Clement is said to be similar to Lucifer, in § 11 of the same chapter even worse than Lucifer, so in our case we may expect something of the same kind. The problem is how the archetypal reading sederit ei, if correct, is to be under- stood. The actual use of sedere in the Rev. does not give any relevant parallel, but Undhagen (p. 340), in discussing Rev. I 34:10 quomodo sedet in corde tuo aut quomodo tibi placet ista tanta dileccio mea, quam habeo ad eam, calls attention to a passage which is of interest for us, too, viz. Deut. 21,14 Si autem postea non sederit 
 
 
 
 
 38 animo tuo, dimittes eam liberam. The sense is here placere; sedere followed by the dative and with this meaning is not unknown in Medieval Latin, as appears from various dictionaries (Be, DuC and Nr), although cases with an impersonal subject seem to predominate. However, it is not evident that 34:22 is necessarily an example of sedere, and the possibility that sederit in our case is a form of sidere should also be taken into consideration. From a semantic point of view, a case like Martial 1,89,5 adeoque penitus sedit hic tibi morbus ('and so deep-seated is this malady of yours', Ker) is then of interest, since something like `even if Lucifer is rooted in him' would suit the context in 34:22 fairly well. An alternative way of accounting for the archetypal sederit ei — which apparently was unacceptable to F Gh — is to consider the possibility that the verb itself — sederit — might be erroneous. Looking in the Rev. for phrases with dyabolus or Lucifer as subject and including a personal dative, I found four possible verbs: adherere, consulere, dominari and suadere. Palaeographically, suadere seems most tempting of these, and if sederit is an error for suaserit, the meaning would be `even if Lucifer will persuade him', which might appear contextually possible. From a semantic point of view, it cannot be denied that adherere, too, is interesting in this connexion, since its meaning is not very far from sederit as interpreted above. But there are other alternatives. Of compounds with sedere, at least considere comes into question (insidere and presidere not existing at all in the Rev., and assidere occurring only in absolute construction), cf.
